Consequently, the Young Turks movement began when these relatively young and newly trained men returned with their education.

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/OttomanNavy.jpg/250px-OttomanNavy.jpg)

The [Ottoman fleet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Navy "Ottoman Navy") in the [Bosphorous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bosporus "Bosporus") near [Ortaköy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ortak%C3%B6y "Ortaköy")

The [Ottoman Navy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Navy "Ottoman Navy") vastly contributed to the expansion of the Empire's territories on the European continent. It initiated the conquest of North Africa, with the addition of [Algeria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algeria "Algeria") and Egypt to the Ottoman Empire in 1517. Starting with the loss of Greece in 1821 and Algeria in 1830, Ottoman naval power and control over the Empire's distant overseas territories began to decline. Sultan [Abdülaziz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abd%C3%BClaziz "Abdülaziz") (reigned 1861–1876) attempted to reestablish a strong Ottoman navy, building the largest fleet after those of Britain and France. The shipyard at Barrow, England, built its first [submarine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Submarine "Submarine") in 1886 for the Ottoman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-first_submarine_at_shipyard_216-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-first_submarine_at_shipyard-216"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>201<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

However, the collapsing Ottoman economy could not sustain the fleet's strength for long. Sultan [Abdülhamid II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abd%C3%BClhamid_II "Abdülhamid II") distrusted the admirals who sided with the reformist [Midhat Pasha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Midhat_Pasha "Midhat Pasha") and claimed that the large and expensive fleet was of no use against the Russians during the Russo-Turkish War. He locked most of the fleet inside the [Golden Horn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_Horn "Golden Horn"), where the ships decayed for the next 30 years. Following the Young Turk Revolution in 1908, the Committee of Union and Progress sought to develop a strong Ottoman naval force. The *Ottoman Navy Foundation* was established in 1910 to buy new ships through public donations.

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Turkish_pilots_in_1912.jpg/250px-Turkish_pilots_in_1912.jpg)

[Ottoman pilots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Aviation_Squadrons "Ottoman Aviation Squadrons") in early 1912

The establishment of [Ottoman military aviation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Air_Force "Ottoman Air Force") dates back to between June 1909 and July 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-217"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>202<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-218"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>203<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The Ottoman Empire started preparing its first pilots and planes, and with the founding of the Aviation School (*Tayyare Mektebi*) in [Yeşilköy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ye%C5%9Filk%C3%B6y "Yeşilköy") on 3 July 1912, the Empire began to tutor its own flight officers. The founding of the Aviation School quickened advancement in the military aviation program, increased the number of enlisted persons within it, and gave the new pilots an active role in the Ottoman Army and Navy. In May 1913, the world's first specialized Reconnaissance Training Program was started by the Aviation School, and the first separate reconnaissance division was established.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In June 1914 a new military academy, the Naval Aviation School (*Bahriye Tayyare Mektebi*) was founded. With the outbreak of World War I, the modernization process stopped abruptly. The [Ottoman Aviation Squadrons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Aviation_Squadrons "Ottoman Aviation Squadrons") fought on many fronts during World War I, from [Galicia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galicia_\(Central_Europe\) "Galicia (Central Europe)") in the west to the Caucasus in the east and [Yemen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yemen "Yemen") in the south.

## Administrative divisions

Main article: [Administrative divisions of the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_the_Ottoman_Empire "Administrative divisions of the Ottoman Empire")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Ottoman_Empire_Administrative_Divisions.png/220px-Ottoman_Empire_Administrative_Divisions.png)

Administrative divisions of the Ottoman Empire in 1899 (year 1317 Hijri)

The Ottoman Empire was first subdivided into provinces, in the sense of fixed territorial units with governors appointed by the sultan, in the late 14th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Imber_219-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Imber-219"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>204<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The [Eyalet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eyalet "Eyalet") (also *Pashalik* or *Beylerbeylik*) was the territory of office of a [Beylerbey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beylerbey "Beylerbey") ("lord of lords" or governor), and was further subdivided into [Sanjaks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanjak "Sanjak").<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-220"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>205<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The [Vilayets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vilayets "Vilayets") were introduced with the promulgation of the "Vilayet Law" (*Teskil-i Vilayet Nizamnamesi*)<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-221"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>206<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> in 1864, as part of the Tanzimat reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-trt_222-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-trt-222"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>207<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Unlike the previous eyalet system, the 1864 law established a hierarchy of administrative units: the vilayet, [liva](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liva_\(sanjak\) "Liva (sanjak)")/[sanjak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanjak "Sanjak")/[mutasarrifate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutasarrifate "Mutasarrifate"), [kaza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaza "Kaza") and [village council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Town_council "Town council"), to which the 1871 Vilayet Law added the [nahiye](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nahiye "Nahiye").<sup id="cite_ref-jpn_223-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-jpn-223"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>208<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

## Economy

Main article: [Economic history of the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_history_of_the_Ottoman_Empire "Economic history of the Ottoman Empire")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Ayd%C4%B1nArchaeologicalMuseum_%28117%29.JPG/250px-Ayd%C4%B1nArchaeologicalMuseum_%28117%29.JPG)

Coins of the Sultanate of Rûm and the Ottoman Empire at [Aydın Archaeological Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ayd%C4%B1n_Archaeological_Museum "Aydın Archaeological Museum")

Ottoman government deliberately pursued a policy for the development of Bursa, Edirne, and Istanbul, successive Ottoman capitals, into major commercial and industrial centers, considering that merchants and artisans were indispensable in creating a new metropolis.<sup id="cite_ref-Inalcik1970209_224-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Inalcik1970209-224"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>209<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> To this end, Mehmed and his successor Bayezid, also encouraged and welcomed migration of the Jews from different parts of Europe, who were settled in Istanbul and other port cities like Salonica. In many places in Europe, Jews were suffering persecution at the hands of their Christian counterparts, such as in Spain, after the conclusion of the [Reconquista](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reconquista "Reconquista"). The tolerance displayed by the Turks was welcomed by the immigrants.

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Mehmed_the_Conqueror_%281432_%E2%80%931481%29.jpg/250px-Mehmed_the_Conqueror_%281432_%E2%80%931481%29.jpg)

A European bronze medal from the period of [Sultan Mehmed the Conqueror](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mehmed_II "Mehmed II"), 1481

The Ottoman economic mind was closely related to the basic concepts of state and society in the Middle East in which the ultimate goal of a state was consolidation and extension of the ruler's power, and the way to reach it was to get rich resources of revenues by making the productive classes prosperous.<sup id="cite_ref-Inalcik1970217_225-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Inalcik1970217-225"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>210<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The ultimate aim was to increase the state revenues without damaging the prosperity of subjects to prevent the emergence of social disorder and to keep the traditional organization of the society intact. The Ottoman economy greatly expanded during the early modern period, with particularly high growth rates during the first half of the eighteenth century. The empire's annual income quadrupled between 1523 and 1748, adjusted for inflation.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-226"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>211<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The organization of the treasury and chancery were developed under the Ottoman Empire more than any other Islamic government and, until the 17th century, they were the leading organization among all their contemporaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Black-2001_202-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Black-2001-202"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>187<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> This organisation developed a scribal bureaucracy (known as "men of the pen") as a distinct group, partly highly trained ulama, which developed into a professional body.<sup id="cite_ref-Black-2001_202-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Black-2001-202"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>187<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The effectiveness of this professional financial body stands behind the success of many great Ottoman statesmen.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-227"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>212<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Ottoman_Banks_Archives_and_Research_Centre.jpg/250px-Ottoman_Banks_Archives_and_Research_Centre.jpg)

The [Ottoman Bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Bank "Ottoman Bank") was founded in 1856 in Constantinople. On 26 August 1896, the bank was [occupied](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occupation_of_the_Ottoman_Bank "Occupation of the Ottoman Bank") by members of the [Armenian Revolutionary Federation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armenian_Revolutionary_Federation "Armenian Revolutionary Federation").

Modern Ottoman studies indicate that the change in relations between the Ottoman Turks and central Europe was caused by the opening of the new sea routes. It is possible to see the decline in the significance of the land routes to the East as Western Europe opened the ocean routes that bypassed the Middle East and the Mediterranean as parallel to the decline of the Ottoman Empire itself.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-228"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>213<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (September 2016)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> The [Anglo-Ottoman Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Ottoman_Treaty "Anglo-Ottoman Treaty"), also known as the [Treaty of Balta Liman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_Balta_Liman "Treaty of Balta Liman") that opened the Ottoman markets directly to English and French competitors, can be seen as one of the staging posts along with this development.

By developing commercial centers and routes, encouraging people to extend the area of cultivated land in the country and international trade through its dominions, the state performed basic economic functions in the Empire. But in all this, the financial and political interests of the state were dominant. Within the social and political system they were living in, Ottoman administrators could not see the desirability of the dynamics and principles of the capitalist and mercantile economies developing in Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Inalcik1970218_229-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Inalcik1970218-229"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>214<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Economic historian [Paul Bairoch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Bairoch "Paul Bairoch") argues that [free trade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_trade "Free trade") contributed to [deindustrialisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deindustrialisation "Deindustrialisation") in the Ottoman Empire. In contrast to the [protectionism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protectionism "Protectionism") of China, Japan, and Spain, the Ottoman Empire had a [liberal trade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_liberalism "Economic liberalism") policy, open to foreign imports. This has origins in [capitulations of the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capitulations_of_the_Ottoman_Empire "Capitulations of the Ottoman Empire"), dating back to the first commercial treaties signed with France in 1536 and taken further with [capitulations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capitulation_\(treaty\) "Capitulation (treaty)") in 1673 and 1740, which lowered [duties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duty_\(economics\) "Duty (economics)") to 3% for imports and exports. The liberal Ottoman policies were praised by British economists, such as [John Ramsay McCulloch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Ramsay_McCulloch "John Ramsay McCulloch") in his *Dictionary of Commerce* (1834), but later criticized by British politicians such as Prime Minister Benjamin Disraeli, who cited the Ottoman Empire as "an instance of the injury done by unrestrained competition" in the 1846 [Corn Laws](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corn_Laws "Corn Laws") debate.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-230"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>215<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

## Demographics

Main article: [Demographics of the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Demographics_of_the_Ottoman_Empire "Demographics of the Ottoman Empire")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ottoman_Smyrna.jpg/250px-Ottoman_Smyrna.jpg)

[İzmir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C4%B0zmir "İzmir") under Ottoman rule in 1900

A population estimate for the empire of 11,692,480 for the 1520–1535 period was obtained by counting the households in Ottoman tithe registers, and multiplying this number by 5.<sup id="cite_ref-Kabadayı_231-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Kabaday%C4%B1-231"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>216<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> For unclear reasons, the population in the 18th century was lower than that in the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-232"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>217<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> An estimate of 7,230,660 for the first census held in 1831 is considered a serious undercount, as this census was meant only to register possible conscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-Kabadayı_231-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Kabaday%C4%B1-231"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>216<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Censuses of Ottoman territories only began in the early 19th century. Figures from 1831 onwards are available as official census results, but the censuses did not cover the whole population. For example, the 1831 census only counted men and did not cover the whole empire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinross1979281_108-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinross1979281-108"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kabadayı_231-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Kabaday%C4%B1-231"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>216<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> For earlier periods estimates of size and distribution of the population are based on observed demographic patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-233"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>218<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Aerial_view_of_the_Galata_Bridge%2C_Istanbul_LCCN2003677084.jpg/250px-Aerial_view_of_the_Galata_Bridge%2C_Istanbul_LCCN2003677084.jpg)

View of [Galata](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galata "Galata") ([Karaköy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karak%C3%B6y "Karaköy")) and the [Galata Bridge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galata_Bridge "Galata Bridge") on the [Golden Horn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_Horn "Golden Horn"), c. 1880–1893

However, it began to rise to reach 25–32 million by 1800, with around 10 million in the European provinces (primarily in the Balkans), 11 million in the Asiatic provinces, and around 3 million in the African provinces. Population densities were higher in the European provinces, double those in Anatolia, which in turn were triple the population densities of Iraq and [Syria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syria "Syria") and five times the population density of Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000110–111_234-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000110%E2%80%93111-234"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>219<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Towards the end of the empire's existence life expectancy was 49 years, compared to the mid-twenties in Serbia at the beginning of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000112_235-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000112-235"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>220<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Epidemic diseases and famine caused major disruption and demographic changes. In 1785 around one-sixth of the Egyptian population died from the plague and Aleppo saw its population reduced by twenty percent in the 18th century. Six famines hit Egypt alone between 1687 and 1731 and the last famine to hit Anatolia was four decades later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000113_236-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000113-236"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>221<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The rise of port cities saw the clustering of populations caused by the development of steamships and railroads. Urbanization increased from 1700 to 1922, with towns and cities growing. Improvements in health and sanitation made them more attractive to live and work in. Port cities like Salonica, in Greece, saw its population rise from 55,000 in 1800 to 160,000 in 1912 and İzmir which had a population of 150,000 in 1800 grew to 300,000 by 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000114_237-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000114-237"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>222<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-238"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>223<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Some regions conversely had population falls—Belgrade saw its population drop from 25,000 to 8,000 mainly due to political strife.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000114_237-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000114-237"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>222<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/20180107_Safranbolu_1945_%2839101010504%29.jpg/250px-20180107_Safranbolu_1945_%2839101010504%29.jpg)

The town of [Safranbolu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Safranbolu "Safranbolu") is one of the best preserved Ottoman villages.

Economic and political migrations made an impact across the empire. For example, the [Russian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Empire "Russian Empire") and Austria-Habsburg annexation of the Crimean and Balkan regions respectively saw large influxes of Muslim refugees—200,000 Crimean Tatars fleeing to Dobruja.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000115_239-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000115-239"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>224<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Between 1783 and 1913, approximately 5–7 million refugees arrived into the Ottoman Empire. Between the 1850s and World War I, about a million North Caucasian Muslims arrived in the Ottoman Empire as refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamed-Troyansky202449_148-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamed-Troyansky202449-148"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Some migrations left indelible marks such as political tension between parts of the empire (e.g., Turkey and Bulgaria), whereas centrifugal effects were noticed in other territories, simpler demographics emerging from diverse populations. Economies were also impacted by the loss of artisans, merchants, manufacturers, and agriculturists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000116_240-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataertSpivey2000116-240"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>225<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Since the 19th century, a large proportion of Muslim peoples from the Balkans emigrated to present-day Turkey. These people are called *[Muhacir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhacir "Muhacir")*.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-241"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>226<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> By the time the Ottoman Empire came to an end in 1922, half of the urban population of Turkey was descended from Muslim refugees from Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google_b_140-5" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-books.google_b-140"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Language

Main article: [Languages of the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Languages_of_the_Ottoman_Empire "Languages of the Ottoman Empire")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1911_Ottoman_Calendar.jpg/250px-1911_Ottoman_Calendar.jpg)

1911 Ottoman calendar shown in several different languages such as: Ottoman Turkish, Greek, Armenian, Hebrew, Bulgarian and French

[Ottoman Turkish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish "Ottoman Turkish") was the official language of the Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-242"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>227<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> It was an [Oghuz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oghuz_languages "Oghuz languages") [Turkic language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkic_languages "Turkic languages") highly influenced by [Persian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_language "Persian language") and [Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic "Arabic"), though lower registries spoken by the common people had fewer influences from other languages compared to higher varieties used by upper classes and governmental authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-243"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>228<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Turkish, in its Ottoman variation, was a language of military and administration since the nascent days of the Ottomans. The Ottoman constitution of 1876 did officially cement the official imperial status of Turkish.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-244"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>229<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The Ottomans had several influential languages: Turkish, spoken by the majority of the people in Anatolia and by the majority of Muslims of the Balkans except some regions such as [Albania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Albania "History of Albania"), [Bosnia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Bosnia "History of Bosnia")<sup id="cite_ref-Bertold_Spuler_page_69_245-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Bertold_Spuler_page_69-245"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>230<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and the [Megleno-Romanian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Megleno-Romanians "Megleno-Romanians")\-inhabited [Nânti](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N%C3%A2nti "Nânti");<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-246"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>231<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Persian, only spoken by the educated;<sup id="cite_ref-Bertold_Spuler_page_69_245-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Bertold_Spuler_page_69-245"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>230<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Arabic, spoken mainly in Egypt, the [Levant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levant "Levant"), [Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Arabia "History of Arabia"), Iraq, North Africa, [Kuwait](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Kuwait "History of Kuwait") and parts of the [Horn of Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horn_of_Africa "Horn of Africa") and [Berber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berber_language "Berber language") in North Africa. In the last two centuries, usage of these became limited, though, and specific: Persian served mainly as a literary language for the educated,<sup id="cite_ref-Bertold_Spuler_page_69_245-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Bertold_Spuler_page_69-245"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>230<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> while [Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic "Arabic") was used for Islamic prayers. In the post-[Tanzimat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tanzimat "Tanzimat") period French became the common Western language among the educated.<sup id="cite_ref-Strauss-2010_23-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Strauss-2010-23"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Because of a low literacy rate among the public (about 2–3% until the early 19th century and just about 15% at the end of the 19th century), ordinary people had to hire [scribes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scribe "Scribe") as "special request-writers" (*arzuhâlci*s) to be able to communicate with the government.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-247"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>232<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Some ethnic groups continued to speak within their families and neighborhoods ([mahalles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mahalle "Mahalle")) with their own languages, though many non-Muslim minorities such as Greeks and Armenians only spoke Turkish.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison196462_248-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison196462-248"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>233<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In villages where two or more populations lived together, the inhabitants often spoke each other's language. In cosmopolitan cities, people often spoke their family languages; many of those who were not ethnic [Turks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish_people "Turkish people") spoke Turkish as a second language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>

### Religion

See also: [Millet (Ottoman Empire)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millet_\(Ottoman_Empire\) "Millet (Ottoman Empire)")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/250px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg)

[Abdülmecid II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdulmejid_II "Abdulmejid II") was the last [caliph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caliphate "Caliphate") of Islam and a member of the [Ottoman dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty "Ottoman dynasty").

[Sunni Islam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunni_Islam "Sunni Islam") was the prevailing *[Dīn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Din_\(Arabic\) "Din (Arabic)")* (customs, legal traditions, and religion) of the Ottoman Empire; the official *[Madh'hab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madhhab "Madhhab")* (school of Islamic [jurisprudence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jurisprudence "Jurisprudence")) was *[Hanafi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanafi "Hanafi").*<sup id="cite_ref-Gunduz_249-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Gunduz-249"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>234<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> From the early 16th century until the early 20th century, the Ottoman sultan also served as the [caliph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caliphate "Caliphate"), or politico-religious leader, of the [Muslim world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslim_world "Muslim world"). Most of the Ottoman Sultans adhered to [Sufism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sufism "Sufism") and followed [Sufi orders](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sufi_orders "Sufi orders"), and believed Sufism was the correct way to reach God.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-250"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>235<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Non-Muslims, particularly Christians and Jews, were present throughout the empire's history. The Ottoman imperial system was charactised by an intricate combination of official Muslim hegemony over non-Muslims and a wide degree of religious tolerance. While religious minorities were never equal under the law, they were granted recognition, protection, and limited freedoms under both Islamic and Ottoman tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-emigrnonm_251-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-emigrnonm-251"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>236<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Until the second half of the 15th century, the majority of Ottoman subjects were Christian.<sup id="cite_ref-Benton-2001_207-4" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Benton-2001-207"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>192<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Non-Muslims remained a significant and economically influential minority, albeit declining significantly by the 19th century, due largely to migration and [secession](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secession "Secession").<sup id="cite_ref-emigrnonm_251-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-emigrnonm-251"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>236<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The proportion of Muslims amounted to 60% in the 1820s, gradually increasing to 69% in the 1870s and 76% in the 1890s.<sup id="cite_ref-emigrnonm_251-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-emigrnonm-251"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>236<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> By 1914, less than a fifth of the empire's population (19.1%) was non-Muslim, mostly made up of Jews and Christian Greeks, Assyrians, and Armenians.<sup id="cite_ref-emigrnonm_251-3" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-emigrnonm-251"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>236<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

#### Islam

Main articles: [Islam in the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islam_in_the_Ottoman_Empire "Islam in the Ottoman Empire"), [Ottoman Caliphate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Caliphate "Ottoman Caliphate"), and [Ottoman persecution of Alevis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_persecution_of_Alevis "Ottoman persecution of Alevis")

See also: [Islam in Turkey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islam_in_Turkey "Islam in Turkey")

[Turkic peoples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkic_peoples "Turkic peoples") practiced a form of [shamanism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shamanism "Shamanism") before adopting Islam. The [Muslim conquest of Transoxiana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Transoxiana "Muslim conquest of Transoxiana") under the [Abbasids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate "Abbasid Caliphate") facilitated the spread of Islam into the Turkic heartland of Central Asia. Many Turkic tribes—including the [Oghuz Turks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oghuz_Turks "Oghuz Turks"), who were the ancestors of both the Seljuks and the Ottomans—gradually converted to Islam and brought religion to Anatolia through their migrations beginning in the 11th century. From its founding, the Ottoman Empire officially supported the [Maturidi school of](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maturidi "Maturidi") [Islamic theology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schools_of_Islamic_theology "Schools of Islamic theology"), which emphasized [human reason](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reason "Reason"), [rationality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rationality "Rationality"), the pursuit of science and [philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_philosophy "Islamic philosophy") (*falsafa*).<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-252"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>237<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-253"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>238<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The Ottomans were among the earliest and most enthusiastic adopters of the [Hanafi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanafi "Hanafi") school of Islamic jurisprudence,<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-254"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>239<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> which was comparatively more flexible and discretionary in its rulings.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-255"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>240<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-256"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>241<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Istanbul_asv2021-10_img15_Y%C4%B1ld%C4%B1z_Hamidiye_Mosque.jpg/250px-Istanbul_asv2021-10_img15_Y%C4%B1ld%C4%B1z_Hamidiye_Mosque.jpg)

The [Yıldız Hamidiye Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Y%C4%B1ld%C4%B1z_Hamidiye_Mosque "Yıldız Hamidiye Mosque") in [Istanbul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Istanbul "Istanbul"), Turkey

The Ottoman Empire had a wide variety of Islamic sects, including [Druze](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Druze "Druze"), [Ismailis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isma%27ilism "Isma'ilism"), [Alevis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alevism "Alevism"), and [Alawites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alawites "Alawites").<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-257"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>242<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Sufism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sufism "Sufism"), a diverse body of Islamic [mysticism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mysticism "Mysticism"), found fertile ground in Ottoman lands; many Sufi religious orders (*[tariqa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tariqa "Tariqa")*), such as the [Bektashi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bektashi_Order "Bektashi Order") and [Mevlevi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mevlevi_Order "Mevlevi Order"), were either established, or saw significant growth, throughout the empire's history.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-258"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>243<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> However, some heterodox Muslim groups were viewed as heretical and even ranked below Jews and Christians in terms of legal protection; Druze were frequent targets of persecution,<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-259"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>244<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> with Ottoman authorities often citing the controversial rulings of [Ibn Taymiyya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyyah "Ibn Taymiyyah"), a member of the conservative [Hanbali school](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanbali "Hanbali").<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-260"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>245<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In 1514, Sultan Selim I ordered the massacre of 40,000 Anatolian Alevis (*[Qizilbash](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qizilbash "Qizilbash")*), whom he considered a [fifth column](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fifth_column "Fifth column") for the rival [Safavid Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Safavid_Iran "Safavid Iran").

During Selim's reign, the Ottoman Empire saw an unprecedented and rapid expansion into the Middle East, particularly the [conquest of the entire Mamluk Sultanate of Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Mamluk_War_\(1516%E2%80%931517\) "Ottoman–Mamluk War (1516–1517)") on the early 16th century. These conquests further solidified the Ottoman claim of being an [Islamic caliphate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caliphate "Caliphate"), although Ottoman sultans had been claiming the title of caliph since the reign of Murad I (1362–1389).<sup id="cite_ref-Lambton-1995_25-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Lambton-1995-25"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The caliphate was officially transferred from the Mamluks to the Ottoman sultanate in 1517, whose members were recognized as caliphs until the [office's abolition on 3 March 1924](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abolition_of_the_Caliphate "Abolition of the Caliphate") by the [Republic of Turkey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grand_National_Assembly_of_Turkey "Grand National Assembly of Turkey") (and the exile of the last caliph, [Abdülmecid II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdulmejid_II "Abdulmejid II"), to France).

#### Christianity and Judaism

Main articles: [Christianity in the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christianity_in_the_Ottoman_Empire "Christianity in the Ottoman Empire") and [History of the Jews in the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_the_Ottoman_Empire "History of the Jews in the Ottoman Empire")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gennadios_II_and_Mehmed_II.jpg/250px-Gennadios_II_and_Mehmed_II.jpg)

[Mehmed the Conqueror](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mehmed_II "Mehmed II") and Patriarch [Gennadius II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gennadius_Scholarius "Gennadius Scholarius")

In accordance with the Muslim *[dhimmi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dhimmi "Dhimmi")* system, the Ottoman Empire guaranteed limited freedoms to Christians, Jews, and other "[people of the book](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/People_of_the_Book "People of the Book")", such as the right to worship, own property, and be exempt from the obligatory alms (*[zakat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zakat "Zakat")*) required of Muslims. However, non-Muslims (or *dhimmi*) were subject to various legal restrictions, including being forbidden to carry weapons, ride on horseback, or have their homes overlook those of Muslims; likewise, they were required to pay higher taxes than Muslim subjects, including the *[jizya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jizya "Jizya"),* which was a key source of state revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-261"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>246<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-262"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>247<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Many Christians and Jews converted to Islam to secure full social and legal status, though most continued to practice their faith without restriction.

The Ottomans developed a unique sociopolitical system known as the [millet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millet_\(Ottoman_Empire\) "Millet (Ottoman Empire)"), which granted non-Muslim communities a large degree of political, legal, and religious autonomy; in essence, members of a millet were subjects of the empire but not subject to the Muslim faith or Islamic law. A millet could govern its own affairs, such as raising taxes and resolving internal legal disputes, with little or no interference from Ottoman authorities, so long as its members were loyal to the sultan and adhered to the rules concerning *dhimmi.* A quintessential example is the ancient Orthodox community of [Mount Athos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mount_Athos "Mount Athos"), which was permitted to retain its autonomy and was never subject to occupation or forced conversion; even special laws were enacted to protect it from outsiders.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-263"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>248<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The [*Rum* Millet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rum_Millet "Rum Millet"), which encompassed most Eastern Orthodox Christians, was governed by the Byzantine-era *[Corpus Juris Civilis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis "Corpus Juris Civilis")* (Code of Justinian), with the [Ecumenical Patriarch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch_of_Constantinople "Ecumenical Patriarch of Constantinople") designated the highest religious and political authority (*millet-bashi*, or [ethnarch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethnarch "Ethnarch")). Likewise, [Ottoman Jews](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_the_Ottoman_Empire "History of the Jews in the Ottoman Empire") came under the authority of the *[Haham Başı](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hakham_Bashi "Hakham Bashi"),* or Ottoman [Chief Rabbi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chief_Rabbi "Chief Rabbi"), while [Armenians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armenians "Armenians") were under the authority of the [chief bishop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catholicos_of_All_Armenians "Catholicos of All Armenians") of the [Armenian Apostolic Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church "Armenian Apostolic Church").<sup id="cite_ref-Syed-2011_264-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Syed-2011-264"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>249<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> As the largest group of non-Muslim subjects, the Rum Millet enjoyed several special privileges in politics and commerce; however, Jews and Armenians were also well represented among the wealthy merchant class, as well as in public administration.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-265"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>250<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-266"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>251<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Some modern scholars consider the millet system to be an early example of [religious pluralism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religious_pluralism "Religious pluralism"), as it accorded minority religious groups official recognition and tolerance.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-267"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>252<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Social-political-religious structure

See also: [Rayah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rayah "Rayah")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Subject_Nationalities_of_the_German_Alliance_%281917%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Subject_Nationalities_of_the_German_Alliance_%281917%29_%28cropped%29.jpg)

Ethnic map of the Ottoman Empire in 1917. Black = Bulgars and Turks, Red = Greeks, Light yellow = Armenians, Blue = Kurds, Orange = Lazes, Dark Yellow = Arabs, Green = Nestorians

Beginning in the early 19th century, society, government, and religion were interrelated in a complex, overlapping way that was deemed inefficient by Atatürk, who systematically dismantled it after 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-268"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>253<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-269"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>254<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In Constantinople, the Sultan ruled two distinct domains: the secular government and the religious hierarchy. Religious officials formed the Ulama, who had control of religious teachings and theology, and also the Empire's judicial system, giving them a major voice in day-to-day affairs in communities across the Empire (but not including the non-Muslim millets). They were powerful enough to reject the military reforms proposed by Sultan [Selim III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selim_III "Selim III"). His successor Sultan [Mahmud II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mahmud_II "Mahmud II") (r. 1808–1839) first won ulama approval before proposing similar reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-270"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>255<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The secularisation program brought by Atatürk ended the ulema and their institutions. The caliphate was abolished, madrasas were closed down, and the sharia courts were abolished. He replaced the Arabic alphabet with Latin letters, ended the religious school system, and gave women some political rights. Many rural traditionalists never accepted this secularisation, and by the 1990s they were reasserting a demand for a larger role for Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-271"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>256<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Istanbul_asv2021-11_img71_StAnthony_of_Padua_Church.jpg/250px-Istanbul_asv2021-11_img71_StAnthony_of_Padua_Church.jpg)

The original [Church of St. Anthony of Padua, Istanbul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Church_of_St._Anthony_of_Padua,_Istanbul "Church of St. Anthony of Padua, Istanbul") was built in 1725 by the local Italian community of Istanbul.

The Janissaries were a highly formidable military unit in the early years, but as Western Europe modernized its military organization technology, the Janissaries became a reactionary force that resisted all change. Steadily the Ottoman military power became outdated, but when the Janissaries felt their privileges were being threatened, or outsiders wanted to modernize them, or they might be superseded by the cavalrymen, they rose in rebellion. The rebellions were highly violent on both sides, but by the time the Janissaries were suppressed, it was far too late for Ottoman military power to catch up with the West.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-272"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>257<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-273"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>258<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The political system was transformed by the destruction of the [Janissaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Janissary "Janissary"), a powerful military/governmental/police force, which revolted in the [Auspicious Incident](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Auspicious_Incident "Auspicious Incident") of 1826. Sultan Mahmud II crushed the revolt, executed the leaders and disbanded the large organization. That set the stage for a slow process of modernization of government functions, as the government sought, with mixed success, to adopt the main elements of Western bureaucracy and military technology.

The Janissaries had been recruited from Christians and other minorities; their abolition enabled the emergence of a Turkish elite to control the Ottoman Empire. A large number of ethnic and religious minorities were tolerated in their own separate segregated domains called millets.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-274"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>259<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> They were primarily [Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Greeks "Ottoman Greeks"), [Armenian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armenians_in_the_Ottoman_Empire "Armenians in the Ottoman Empire"), or [Jewish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_the_Ottoman_Empire "History of the Jews in the Ottoman Empire"). In each locality, they governed themselves, spoke their own language, ran their own schools, cultural and religious institutions, and paid somewhat higher taxes. They had no power outside the millet. The Imperial government protected them and prevented major violent clashes between ethnic groups.

Ethnic nationalism, based on distinctive religion and language, provided a centripetal force that eventually destroyed the Ottoman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataert1983_275-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataert1983-275"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>260<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In addition, Muslim ethnic groups, which were not part of the millet system, especially the Arabs and the Kurds, were outside the Turkish culture and developed their own separate nationalism. The British sponsored Arab nationalism in the First World War, promising an independent Arab state in return for Arab support. Most Arabs supported the Sultan, but those near Mecca believed in and supported the British promise.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-276"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>261<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Istanbul_asv2021-11_img03_Hemdat_Israel_Synagogue.jpg/250px-Istanbul_asv2021-11_img03_Hemdat_Israel_Synagogue.jpg)

[Hemdat Israel Synagogue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemdat_Israel_Synagogue "Hemdat Israel Synagogue") of [Istanbul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Istanbul "Istanbul")

At the local level, power was held beyond the control of the Sultan by the [ayans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ayan_\(class\) "Ayan (class)") or local notables. The ayan collected taxes, formed local armies to compete with other notables, took a reactionary attitude toward political or economic change, and often defied policies handed down by the Sultan.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-277"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>262<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

After the 18th century, the Ottoman Empire was shrinking, as Russia put on heavy pressure and expanded to its south; Egypt became effectively independent in 1805, and the British later took it over, along with Cyprus. Greece became independent, and Serbia and other Balkan areas became highly restive as the force of nationalism pushed against imperialism. The French took over Algeria and Tunisia. The Europeans all thought that the empire was a sick man in rapid decline. Only the Germans seemed helpful, and their support led to the Ottoman Empire joining the central powers in 1915, with the result that they came out as one of the heaviest losers of the First World War in 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-278"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>263<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

## Culture

Main article: [Culture of the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Culture_of_the_Ottoman_Empire "Culture of the Ottoman Empire")
