![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/WaldmeierLebanon.gif/250px-WaldmeierLebanon.gif)

Depiction of a [hookah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hookah "Hookah") shop in [Lebanon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebanon "Lebanon")

The Ottomans absorbed some of the traditions, art, and institutions of cultures in the regions they conquered and added new dimensions to them. Numerous traditions and cultural traits of previous empires (in fields such as architecture, cuisine, music, leisure, and government) were adopted by the Ottoman Turks, who developed them into new forms, resulting in a new and distinctively Ottoman cultural identity. Although the predominant literary language of the Ottoman Empire was Turkish, Persian was the preferred vehicle for the projection of an imperial image.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-279"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>264<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

[Slavery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavery_in_the_Ottoman_Empire "Slavery in the Ottoman Empire") was part of Ottoman society,<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-280"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>265<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> with most slaves employed as domestic servants. Agricultural slavery, like that in the Americas, was relatively rare. Unlike systems of [chattel slavery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavery "Slavery"), slaves under Islamic law were not regarded as movable property, and the children of female slaves were born legally free. Female slaves were still sold in the Empire as late as 1908.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-281"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>266<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> During the 19th century the Empire came under pressure from Western European countries to outlaw the practice. Policies developed by various sultans throughout the 19th century attempted to curtail the [Ottoman slave trade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_slave_trade "Ottoman slave trade") but slavery had centuries of religious backing and sanction and so was never abolished in the Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-Syed-2011_264-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Syed-2011-264"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>249<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Ottomans adopted Persian bureaucratic traditions and culture. The sultans also made an important contribution in the development of Persian literature.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-282"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>267<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Language was not an obvious sign of group connection and identity in the 16th century among the rulers of the Ottoman Empire, [Safavid Iran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Safavid_Iran "Safavid Iran") and [Abu'l-Khayrid Shibanids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shaybanids "Shaybanids") of [Central Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Asia "Central Asia").<sup id="cite_ref-Comstock-Skipp-2023_283-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Comstock-Skipp-2023-283"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>268<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Hence the ruling classes of all three polities were bilingual in varieties of Persian and Turkic.<sup id="cite_ref-Comstock-Skipp-2023_283-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Comstock-Skipp-2023-283"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>268<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> But in the century's final quarter, linguistic adjustments occurred in the Ottoman and Safavid realms defined by a new rigidity that favoured Ottoman Turkish and Persian, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Comstock-Skipp-2023_283-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Comstock-Skipp-2023-283"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>268<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Education

Main article: [Education in the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Education_in_the_Ottoman_Empire "Education in the Ottoman Empire")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_%2814667026514%29.jpg/250px-Beyaz%C4%B1t_State_Library_%2814667026514%29.jpg)

The Beyazıt State Library was founded in 1884.

In the Ottoman Empire, each [millet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millet_\(Ottoman_Empire\) "Millet (Ottoman Empire)") established a schooling system serving its members.<sup id="cite_ref-Strauss-2016_284-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Strauss-2016-284"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>269<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Education was therefore largely divided on ethnic and religious lines: few non-Muslims attended schools for Muslim students, and vice versa. Most institutions that served all ethnic and religious groups taught in French or other languages.<sup id="cite_ref-StraussPT195_285-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-StraussPT195-285"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>270<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Several "foreign schools" (*Frerler mektebleri*) operated by religious clergy primarily served Christians, although some Muslim students attended.<sup id="cite_ref-Strauss-2016_284-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Strauss-2016-284"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>269<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Garnett described the schools for Christians and Jews as "organised upon European models", with "voluntary contributions" supporting their operation and most of them "well attended" and with "a high standard of education".<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-286"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>271<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Literature

Main article: [Ottoman literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_literature "Ottoman literature")

The two primary streams of Ottoman written literature are poetry and [prose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prose "Prose"). Poetry was by far the dominant stream. The earliest work of Ottoman historiography for example, the [*İskendernâme*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iskendername "Iskendername"), was composed by the poet [Taceddin Ahmedi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taceddin_Ahmedi "Taceddin Ahmedi").<sup id="cite_ref-:1_287-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-:1-287"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>272<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Until the 19th century, Ottoman prose did not contain any examples of fiction: there were no counterparts to, for instance, the European [romance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chivalric_romance "Chivalric romance"), short story, or novel. Analog genres did exist, though, in both [Turkish folk literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish_folk_literature "Turkish folk literature") and in [Divan poetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diwan_\(poetry\) "Diwan (poetry)").

Ottoman Divan poetry was a highly ritualized and symbolic art form. From the [Persian poetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_literature "Persian literature") that largely inspired it, it inherited a wealth of symbols whose meanings and interrelationships—both of similitude (مراعات نظير *mura'ât-i nazîr* / تناسب *tenâsüb*) and opposition (تضاد *tezâd*) were more or less prescribed. Divan poetry was composed through the constant juxtaposition of many such images within a strict metrical framework, allowing numerous potential meanings to emerge. The vast majority of Divan poetry was [lyric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lyric_poetry "Lyric poetry") in nature: either [gazels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gazel "Gazel") (which make up the greatest part of the repertoire of the tradition), or kasîdes. But there were other common genres, especially the mesnevî, a kind of [verse romance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Courtly_romance "Courtly romance") and thus a variety of [narrative poetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Narrative_poetry "Narrative poetry"); the two most notable examples of this form are the [Leyli and Majnun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leyli_and_Majnun_\(Fuzuli\) "Leyli and Majnun (Fuzuli)") of [Fuzuli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fuzuli_\(poet\) "Fuzuli (poet)") and the [Hüsn ü Aşk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H%C3%BCsn_%C3%BC_A%C5%9Fk "Hüsn ü Aşk") of [Şeyh Gâlib](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C5%9Eeyh_G%C3%A2lib "Şeyh Gâlib"). The [Seyahatnâme](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seyahatn%C3%A2me "Seyahatnâme") of [Evliya Çelebi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evliya_%C3%87elebi "Evliya Çelebi") (1611–1682) is an outstanding example of travel literature.

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG/250px-Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG)

[Ahmet Nedîm Efendi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ned%C3%AEm "Nedîm"), one of the most celebrated Ottoman poets

Until the 19th century, [Ottoman prose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prose_of_the_Ottoman_Empire "Prose of the Ottoman Empire") did not develop to the extent that contemporary Divan poetry did. A large part of the reason was that much prose was expected to adhere to the rules of *sec* (سجع, also transliterated as *seci*), or [rhymed prose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhymed_prose "Rhymed prose"),<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-288"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>273<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> a type of writing descended from the Arabic [saj'](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saj%27 "Saj'") that prescribed that between each adjective and noun in a string of words, such as a sentence, there must be a rhyme. Nevertheless, there was a tradition of prose in the literature of the time, though it was exclusively nonfictional. One apparent exception was [Muhayyelât](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giritli_Ali_Aziz_Efendi "Giritli Ali Aziz Efendi") (*Fancies*) by [Giritli Ali Aziz Efendi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giritli_Ali_Aziz_Efendi "Giritli Ali Aziz Efendi"), a collection of stories of the fantastic written in 1796, though not published until 1867. The first novel published in the Ottoman Empire was [Vartan Pasha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vartan_Pasha "Vartan Pasha")'s 1851 *The Story of Akabi* (Turkish: *Akabi Hikyayesi*). It was written in Turkish but with [Armenian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armenian_language "Armenian language") script.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-289"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>274<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-290"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>275<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-291"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>276<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-292"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>277<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Due to historically close ties with France, [French literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_literature "French literature") constituted the major Western influence on Ottoman literature in the latter half of the 19th century. As a result, many of the same movements prevalent in France during this period had Ottoman equivalents; in the developing Ottoman prose tradition, for instance, the influence of [Romanticism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanticism "Romanticism") can be seen during the Tanzimat period, and that of the [Realist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Realism_\(arts\) "Realism (arts)") and [Naturalist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturalism_\(literature\) "Naturalism (literature)") movements in subsequent periods; in the poetic tradition, on the other hand, the influence of the [Symbolist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbolism_\(arts\) "Symbolism (arts)") and [Parnassian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parnassianism "Parnassianism") movements was paramount.

Many of the writers in the Tanzimat period wrote in several different genres simultaneously. This diversity was, in part, due to Tanzimat writers' wish to disseminate as much of the new literature as possible, in the hopes that it would contribute to a revitalization of Ottoman [social structures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_structure "Social structure").<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-293"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>278<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Media

Main article: [Media of the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Media_of_the_Ottoman_Empire "Media of the Ottoman Empire")

The media of the Ottoman Empire was diverse, with newspapers and journals published in languages including [French](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_\(language\) "French (language)"),<sup id="cite_ref-BaruhMusnik_294-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-BaruhMusnik-294"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>279<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_\(language\) "Greek (language)"),<sup id="cite_ref-StraussConstp32_295-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-StraussConstp32-295"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>280<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and [German](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_\(language\) "German (language)").<sup id="cite_ref-Syed-2011_264-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Syed-2011-264"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>249<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Many of these publications were centered in [Constantinople](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constantinople "Constantinople"),<sup id="cite_ref-Kendallp339_296-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Kendallp339-296"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>281<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> but there were also French-language newspapers produced in [Beirut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beirut "Beirut"), [Salonika](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salonika "Salonika"), and [Smyrna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smyrna "Smyrna").<sup id="cite_ref-StraussLanguagep122_297-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-StraussLanguagep122-297"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>282<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Non-Muslim ethnic minorities in the empire used French as a [lingua franca](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lingua_franca "Lingua franca") and used French-language publications,<sup id="cite_ref-BaruhMusnik_294-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-BaruhMusnik-294"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>279<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> while some provincial newspapers were published in [Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic "Arabic").<sup id="cite_ref-StraussConstp25_298-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-StraussConstp25-298"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>283<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The use of French in the media persisted until the [end of the empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Ottoman_Empire "Dissolution of the Ottoman Empire") in 1923 and for a few years thereafter in the [Republic of Turkey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkey "Turkey").<sup id="cite_ref-BaruhMusnik_294-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-BaruhMusnik-294"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>279<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Architecture

Main article: [Ottoman architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_architecture "Ottoman architecture")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg/250px-Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg)

[Süleymaniye Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/S%C3%BCleymaniye_Mosque "Süleymaniye Mosque") in Istanbul, designed by [Mimar Sinan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mimar_Sinan "Mimar Sinan") in the 16th century and a major example of the [classical Ottoman style](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_Ottoman_architecture "Classical Ottoman architecture")

The architecture of the empire developed from earlier [Seljuk Turkish architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatolian_Seljuk_architecture "Anatolian Seljuk architecture"), with influences from [Byzantine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Byzantine_architecture "Byzantine architecture") and [Iranian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iranian_architecture "Iranian architecture") architecture and other architectural traditions in the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-299"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>284<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bloom-2009_300-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Bloom-2009-300"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>285<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Freely-2011_301-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Freely-2011-301"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>286<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Early Ottoman architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Ottoman_architecture "Early Ottoman architecture") experimented with multiple building types over the course of the 13th to 15th centuries, progressively evolving into the [classical Ottoman style](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_Ottoman_architecture "Classical Ottoman architecture") of the 16th and 17th centuries, which was also strongly influenced by the [Hagia Sophia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hagia_Sophia "Hagia Sophia").<sup id="cite_ref-Freely-2011_301-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Freely-2011-301"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>286<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Goodwin_302-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Goodwin-302"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>287<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The most important architect of the Classical period is [Mimar Sinan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mimar_Sinan "Mimar Sinan"), whose major works include the [Şehzade Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C5%9Eehzade_Mosque "Şehzade Mosque"), [Süleymaniye Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/S%C3%BCleymaniye_Mosque "Süleymaniye Mosque"), and [Selimiye Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selimiye_Mosque,_Edirne "Selimiye Mosque, Edirne").<sup id="cite_ref-AgostonMasters20102_303-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-AgostonMasters20102-303"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>288<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grove_encycl-Ottoman_304-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Grove_encycl-Ottoman-304"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>289<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The second half of the 16th century also saw the apogee of some [types of decoration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_decoration "Ottoman decoration"), most notably in the production of [Iznik tiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iznik_pottery "Iznik pottery").<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-305"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>290<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Mihrisah_Sultan_Complex_Eyup_DSCF7829.jpg/250px-Mihrisah_Sultan_Complex_Eyup_DSCF7829.jpg)

[Sebil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sebil "Sebil") (water distribution kiosk) of the [Mihrişah Sultan Complex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mihri%C5%9Fah_Sultan_Complex "Mihrişah Sultan Complex") in Istanbul, from the late 18th century, an example of the [Ottoman Baroque style](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Baroque_style "Ottoman Baroque style")

Beginning in the 18th century, Ottoman architecture was opened to external influences, particularly from [Baroque architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baroque_architecture "Baroque architecture") in Western Europe, which eventually gave rise to the [Ottoman Baroque](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Baroque_architecture "Ottoman Baroque architecture") style that emerged in the 1740s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERüstem201921–70_(see_chapters_1_and_2_generally)_306-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTER%C3%BCstem201921%E2%80%9370_(see_chapters_1_and_2_generally)-306"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>291<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The [Nuruosmaniye Mosque](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuruosmaniye_Mosque "Nuruosmaniye Mosque") is one of the most important examples from this period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreely2011355_307-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreely2011355-307"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>292<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010526_308-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010526-308"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>293<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> During the 19th century, influences from Western Europe became more prominent, brought in by architects such as those from the [Balyan family](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balyan_family "Balyan family").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393_309-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393-309"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>294<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Empire style](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empire_style "Empire style") and [Neoclassical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture "Neoclassical architecture") motifs were introduced and a trend towards [eclecticism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eclecticism_in_architecture "Eclecticism in architecture") was evident in many types of buildings, such as the [Dolmabaçe Palace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace "Dolmabahçe Palace").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010605–606_310-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010605%E2%80%93606-310"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>295<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The last decades of the Ottoman Empire also saw the development of a new architectural style called neo-Ottoman or Ottoman [revivalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revivalism_\(architecture\) "Revivalism (architecture)"), also known as the [First National Architectural Movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_national_architectural_movement "First national architectural movement"),<sup id="cite_ref-Bloom-2009a_311-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Bloom-2009a-311"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>296<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> by architects such as [Mimar Kemaleddin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mimar_Kemaleddin "Mimar Kemaleddin") and [Vedat Tek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vedat_Tek "Vedat Tek").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393_309-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreely2011393-309"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>294<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Ottoman dynastic patronage was concentrated in the historic capitals of Bursa, Edirne, and Istanbul (Constantinople), as well as in several other important administrative centers such as [Amasya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amasya "Amasya") and [Manisa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manisa "Manisa"). It was in these centers that most important developments in Ottoman architecture occurred and that the most monumental Ottoman architecture can be found.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010679_312-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010679-312"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>297<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Major religious monuments were typically architectural complexes, known as a *[külliye](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K%C3%BClliye "Külliye")*, that had multiple components providing different services or amenities. In addition to a mosque, these could include a [madrasa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madrasa "Madrasa"), a [hammam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hammam "Hammam"), an [imaret](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imaret "Imaret"), a [sebil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sebil_\(fountain\) "Sebil (fountain)"), a market, a [caravanserai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caravanserai "Caravanserai"), a [primary school](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuttab "Kuttab"), or others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010_313-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010-313"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>298<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> These complexes were governed and managed with the help of a *[vakıf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vak%C4%B1f "Vakıf")* agreement (Arabic *waqf*).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010_313-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010-313"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>298<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Ottoman constructions were still abundant in Anatolia and in the Balkans (Rumelia), but in the more distant Middle Eastern and North African provinces older [Islamic architectural](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_architecture "Islamic architecture") styles continued to hold strong influence and were sometimes blended with Ottoman styles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuban2010571–596_314-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuban2010571%E2%80%93596-314"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>299<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlairBloom1995251_315-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlairBloom1995251-315"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>300<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Decorative arts

Further information: [Ottoman illumination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_illumination "Ottoman illumination") and [Ottoman miniature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_miniature "Ottoman miniature")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg/250px-Topkapi_Sarayi_in_time_of_Selim_I_-_left.jpg)

[Ottoman miniature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_miniature "Ottoman miniature") lost its function with the Westernization of Ottoman culture.

The tradition of [Ottoman miniatures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_miniature "Ottoman miniature"), painted to illustrate manuscripts or used in dedicated albums, was heavily influenced by the [Persian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_miniature "Persian miniature") art form, though it also included elements of the [Byzantine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Byzantine_art "Byzantine art") tradition of [illumination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript "Illuminated manuscript") and painting.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-316"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>301<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> A Greek academy of painters, the *Nakkashane-i-Rum*, was established in the [Topkapi Palace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topkap%C4%B1_Palace "Topkapı Palace") in the 15th century, while early in the following century a similar Persian academy, the *Nakkashane-i-Irani*, was added. [Surname-i Hümayun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surname-i_H%C3%BCmayun "Surname-i Hümayun") (Imperial Festival Books) were albums that commemorated celebrations in the Ottoman Empire in pictorial and textual detail.

[Ottoman illumination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_illumination "Ottoman illumination") covers non-figurative painted or drawn decorative art in books or on sheets in *[muraqqa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muraqqa "Muraqqa")* or albums, as opposed to the figurative images of the [Ottoman miniature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_miniature "Ottoman miniature"). It was a part of the Ottoman Book Arts together with the Ottoman miniature (*taswir*), calligraphy (*hat*), [Islamic calligraphy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy "Islamic calligraphy"), bookbinding (*cilt*) and [paper marbling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paper_marbling "Paper marbling") (*ebru*). In the Ottoman Empire, [illuminated and illustrated manuscripts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript "Illuminated manuscript") were commissioned by the Sultan or the administrators of the court. In Topkapi Palace, these manuscripts were created by the artists working in *Nakkashane*, the atelier of the miniature and illumination artists. Both religious and non-religious books could be illuminated. Also, sheets for albums *levha* consisted of illuminated calligraphy (*hat*) of *[tughra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tughra "Tughra")*, religious texts, verses from poems or proverbs, and purely decorative drawings.

The art of carpet [weaving](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weaving "Weaving") was particularly significant in the Ottoman Empire, carpets having an immense importance both as decorative furnishings, rich in religious and other symbolism and as a practical consideration, as it was customary to remove one's shoes in living quarters.<sup id="cite_ref-foroqhi_317-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-foroqhi-317"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>302<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The weaving of such carpets originated in the [nomadic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nomad "Nomad") cultures of central Asia (carpets being an easily transportable form of furnishing), and eventually spread to the settled societies of Anatolia. Turks used carpets, rugs, and [kilims](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kilim "Kilim") not just on the floors of a room but also as a hanging on walls and doorways, where they provided additional insulation. They were also commonly donated to mosques, which often amassed large collections of them.<sup id="cite_ref-foroqhip153_318-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-foroqhip153-318"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>303<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Music and performing arts

Further information: [Ottoman Music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Music "Ottoman Music")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Musicians_and_dancers_from_ottoman_empire.jpg/250px-Musicians_and_dancers_from_ottoman_empire.jpg)

Musicians and dancers entertaining the crowds, from the [Surname-i Hümayun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surname-i_H%C3%BCmayun "Surname-i Hümayun"), 1720

[Ottoman classical music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_music "Ottoman music") was an important part of the education of the Ottoman elite. A number of the Ottoman sultans have accomplished musicians and composers themselves, such as [Selim III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selim_III "Selim III"), whose compositions are often still performed today. Ottoman classical music arose largely from a confluence of [Byzantine music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Byzantine_music "Byzantine music"), [Armenian music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Music_of_Armenia "Music of Armenia"), [Arabic music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_music "Arabic music"), and [Persian music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_traditional_music "Persian traditional music"). Compositionally, it is organized around rhythmic units called [usul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Usul_\(music\) "Usul (music)"), which are somewhat similar to [meter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metre_\(music\) "Metre (music)") in Western music, and [melodic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melody "Melody") units called [makam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish_makam "Turkish makam"), which bear some resemblance to Western [musical modes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mode_\(music\) "Mode (music)").

The [instruments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Musical_instrument "Musical instrument") used are a mixture of Anatolian and Central Asian instruments (the [saz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ba%C4%9Flama "Bağlama"), the [bağlama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ba%C4%9Flama "Bağlama"), the [kemence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kemenche "Kemenche")), other Middle Eastern instruments (the [ud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oud "Oud"), the [tanbur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tanbur "Tanbur"), the [kanun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qanun_\(instrument\) "Qanun (instrument)"), the [ney](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ney "Ney")), and—later in the tradition—Western instruments (the violin and the piano). Because of a geographic and cultural divide between the capital and other areas, two broadly distinct styles of music arose in the Ottoman Empire: Ottoman classical music and folk music. In the provinces, several different kinds of [folk music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Folk_music "Folk music") were created. The most dominant regions with their distinguished musical styles are Balkan-Thracian Türküs, North-Eastern ([Laz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laz_people "Laz people")) Türküs, Aegean Türküs, Central Anatolian Türküs, Eastern Anatolian Türküs, and Caucasian Türküs. Some of the distinctive styles were: [Janissary music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_military_band "Ottoman military band"), [Roma music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romani_music "Romani music"), [Belly dance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belly_dance "Belly dance"), [Turkish folk music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish_folk_music "Turkish folk music").

The traditional [shadow play](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shadow_play "Shadow play") called [Karagöz and Hacivat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karag%C3%B6z_and_Hacivat "Karagöz and Hacivat") was widespread throughout the Ottoman Empire and featured characters representing all of the major ethnic and social groups in that culture.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-319"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>304<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-320"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>305<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> It was performed by a single puppet master, who voiced all of the characters, and accompanied by [tambourine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tambourine "Tambourine") (*def*). Its origins are obscure, deriving perhaps from an older Egyptian tradition, or possibly from an Asian source.

### Cuisine

Main article: [Ottoman cuisine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_cuisine "Ottoman cuisine")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg/250px-Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg)

Turkish women baking bread, 1790

[Ottoman cuisine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_cuisine "Ottoman cuisine") is the cuisine of the capital, Constantinople ([Istanbul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Istanbul "Istanbul")), and the regional capital cities, where the melting pot of cultures created a common cuisine that most of the population regardless of ethnicity shared. This diverse cuisine was honed in the Imperial Palace's kitchens by chefs brought from certain parts of the Empire to create and experiment with different ingredients. The creations of the Ottoman Palace's kitchens filtered to the population, for instance through [Ramadan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ramadan "Ramadan") events, and through the cooking at the [Yalıs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yal%C4%B1 "Yalı") of the [Pashas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pasha "Pasha"), and from there on spread to the rest of the population.

Much of the cuisine of former Ottoman territories today is descended from a shared Ottoman cuisine, especially [Turkish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish_cuisine "Turkish cuisine"), and including [Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_cuisine "Greek cuisine"), [Balkan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balkan_cuisine "Balkan cuisine"), [Armenian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armenian_cuisine "Armenian cuisine"), and [Middle Eastern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Eastern_cuisine "Middle Eastern cuisine") cuisines.<sup id="cite_ref-Fragner_321-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Fragner-321"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>306<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Sports

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/MedyaBJK.jpg/250px-MedyaBJK.jpg)

Members of [Beşiktaş J.K.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Be%C5%9Fikta%C5%9F_J.K. "Beşiktaş J.K.") in 1903

The main sports Ottomans were engaged in were [Turkish wrestling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wrestling_in_Turkey "Wrestling in Turkey"), hunting, [Turkish archery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish_archery "Turkish archery"), horseback riding, [equestrian javelin throw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jereed "Jereed"), arm wrestling, and swimming. European model sports clubs were formed with the spreading popularity of [football](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Association_football "Association football") matches in 19th century Constantinople. The leading clubs, according to timeline, were [Beşiktaş Gymnastics Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Be%C5%9Fikta%C5%9F_J.K. "Beşiktaş J.K.") (1903), [Galatasaray Sports Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galatasaray_S.K. "Galatasaray S.K.") (1905), [Fenerbahçe Sports Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fenerbah%C3%A7e_S.K. "Fenerbahçe S.K.") (1907), [MKE Ankaragücü (formerly Turan Sanatkarangücü)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MKE_Ankarag%C3%BCc%C3%BC "MKE Ankaragücü") (1910) in Constantinople. Football clubs were formed in other provinces too, such as [Karşıyaka Sports Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kar%C5%9F%C4%B1yaka_S.K. "Karşıyaka S.K.") (1912), [Altay Sports Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Altay_S.K. "Altay S.K.") (1914) and [Turkish Fatherland Football Club](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%9Clk%C3%BCspor "Ülküspor") (later [Ülküspor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%9Clk%C3%BCspor "Ülküspor")) (1914) of [İzmir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C4%B0zmir "İzmir").

## Science and technology

Main article: [Science and technology in the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_the_Ottoman_Empire "Science and technology in the Ottoman Empire")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg/250px-Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg)

The [Constantinople observatory of Taqi ad-Din](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constantinople_observatory_of_Taqi_ad-Din "Constantinople observatory of Taqi ad-Din") in 1577

Over the course of Ottoman history, the Ottomans managed to build a large collection of libraries complete with translations of books from other cultures, as well as original manuscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-Ágoston_and_Alan_Masters583_73-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-%C3%81goston_and_Alan_Masters583-73"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> A great part of this desire for local and foreign manuscripts arose in the 15th century. [Sultan Mehmet II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mehmed_II "Mehmed II") ordered [Georgios Amiroutzes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Amiroutzes "George Amiroutzes"), a Greek scholar from [Trabzon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trabzon "Trabzon"), to translate and make available to Ottoman educational institutions the geography book of [Ptolemy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ptolemy "Ptolemy"). Another example is [Ali Qushji](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ali_Qushji "Ali Qushji") – an [astronomer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomy_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world "Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world"), [mathematician](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world "Mathematics in the medieval Islamic world") and [physicist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physics_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world "Physics in the medieval Islamic world") originally from [Samarkand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samarkand "Samarkand") – who became a professor in two madrasas and influenced Ottoman circles as a result of his writings and the activities of his students, even though he only spent two or three years in Constantinople before his death.<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-322"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>307<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

[Taqi al-Din](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taqi_ad-Din_Muhammad_ibn_Ma%27ruf "Taqi ad-Din Muhammad ibn Ma'ruf") built the [Constantinople observatory of Taqi ad-Din](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constantinople_observatory_of_Taqi_ad-Din "Constantinople observatory of Taqi ad-Din") in 1577, where he carried out observations until 1580. He calculated the [eccentricity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity "Orbital eccentricity") of the Sun's orbit and the annual motion of the [apogee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apsis "Apsis").<sup id="cite_ref-Tekeli_323-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Tekeli-323"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>308<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> However, the observatory's primary purpose was almost certainly [astrological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrology "Astrology") rather than astronomical, leading to its destruction in 1580 due to the rise of a clerical faction that opposed its use for that purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-324"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>309<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> He also experimented with [steam power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steam_engine "Steam engine") in [Ottoman Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egypt_Eyalet "Egypt Eyalet") in 1551, when he described a [steam jack](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roasting_jack "Roasting jack") driven by a rudimentary [steam turbine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steam_turbine "Steam turbine").<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-325"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>310<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Young_woman_reading_%281880%29%2C_by_Osman_Hamdi_Bey.jpg/250px-Young_woman_reading_%281880%29%2C_by_Osman_Hamdi_Bey.jpg)

*Girl Reciting the Qurān* (*Kuran Okuyan Kız*), an 1880 painting by the Ottoman polymath [Osman Hamdi Bey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osman_Hamdi_Bey "Osman Hamdi Bey"), whose works often showed women engaged in educational activities<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-326"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>311<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

[Şerafeddin Sabuncuoğlu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sabuncuo%C4%9Flu_%C5%9Eerafeddin "Sabuncuoğlu Şerafeddin") was the author of the first surgical atlas and the last major [medical encyclopaedia from the Islamic world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medicine_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world "Medicine in the medieval Islamic world").<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-327"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>312<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The Ottoman Empire is credited with the invention of several surgical instruments in use such as [forceps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forceps "Forceps"), [catheters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catheter "Catheter"), [scalpels and lancets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scalpel "Scalpel") as well as [pincers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pincers_\(tool\) "Pincers (tool)").<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-328"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>313<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (April 2022)">better&nbsp;source&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup>

In the early 19th century, [Egypt under Muhammad Ali](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Egypt_under_the_Muhammad_Ali_dynasty "History of Egypt under the Muhammad Ali dynasty") began using [steam engines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steam_engine "Steam engine") for industrial manufacturing, with industries such as [ironworks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ironworks "Ironworks"), [textile manufacturing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Textile_manufacturing "Textile manufacturing"), [paper mills](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paper_mill "Paper mill") and [hulling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Husk "Husk") mills moving towards steam power.<sup id="cite_ref-batou193_329-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-batou193-329"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>314<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Economic historian Jean Batou argues that the necessary economic conditions existed in Egypt for the adoption of [oil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oil "Oil") as a potential energy source for its steam engines later in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-batou193_329-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-batou193-329"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>314<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Footnotes

1. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-5 "Jump up")** *[İslâm Ansiklopedisi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C4%B0sl%C3%A2m_Ansiklopedisi "İslâm Ansiklopedisi")*: "It is disputed when the Ottomans conquered this place; Various dates have been put forward in this regard, such as 1361, 1362, 1367 and 1369. Among these, the opinion that Edirne was captured in 1361 as a result of a systematic conquest policy by Murad and Lala Şahin, while Orhan Gazi was still alive, gains prominence. However, it has also been stated that the date of conquest may have occurred after 1366 (1369), based on an elegy showing that the city metropolitan Polykarpos was in Edirne in this capacity until 1366.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
2. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-9 "Jump up")** In Ottoman Turkish, the city was known by various names, among which were *Ḳosṭanṭīnīye* (قسطنطينيه) (replacing the suffix *\-polis* with the Arabic suffix), *Istanbul* (استنبول) and *Islambol* (اسلامبول, lit. 'full of Islam'); see [Names of Istanbul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Names_of_Istanbul "Names of Istanbul")). Kostantiniyye became obsolete in Turkish after the [proclamation of the Republic of Turkey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proclamation_of_the_Republic_of_Turkey "Proclamation of the Republic of Turkey") in 1923,<sup id="cite_ref-Edhemp286_6-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Edhemp286-6"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and after Turkey's transition to Latin script in 1928,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> the Turkish government in 1930 requested that foreign embassies and companies use *Istanbul*, and that name became widely accepted internationally.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
3. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-10 "Jump up")** [Liturgical language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liturgical_language "Liturgical language"); among Arabic-speaking citizens
4. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-19 "Jump up")** Court, diplomacy, poetry, historiographical works, literary works, taught in state schools, and offered as an elective course or recommended for study in some *[madrasas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madrasa "Madrasa")*.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
5. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-20 "Jump up")** Among Greek-speaking community; spoken by some sultans.
6. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-22 "Jump up")** Decrees in the 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
7. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-24 "Jump up")** Foreign language among educated people in the post-[Tanzimat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tanzimat "Tanzimat")/late imperial period.<sup id="cite_ref-Strauss-2010_23-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Strauss-2010-23"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
8. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-26 "Jump up")** The sultan from 1512 to 1520.
9. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-27 "Jump up")** In 1922, the Ottoman Empire came to an end with the [abolition of the monarchy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abolition_of_the_Ottoman_sultanate "Abolition of the Ottoman sultanate"), and Abdülmecid, a member of the [Ottoman dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty "Ottoman dynasty"), was elected caliph by the Grand National Assembly of Turkey to replace the last sultan Mehmed VI and held the title under the Ankara Government (1922–1923) and the Republic (1923–1924).
10. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-29 "Jump up")** 1 November 1922 marks the formal ending of the Ottoman Empire. Mehmed VI departed Constantinople on 17 November 1922.
11. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-30 "Jump up")** The [Treaty of Sèvres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_S%C3%A8vres "Treaty of Sèvres") (10 August 1920) afforded a small existence to the Ottoman Empire. On 1 November 1922, the [Grand National Assembly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grand_National_Assembly_of_Turkey "Grand National Assembly of Turkey") (GNAT) abolished the sultanate and declared that all the deeds of the Ottoman regime in Constantinople were null and void as of 16 March 1920, the date of the [occupation of Constantinople](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occupation_of_Constantinople "Occupation of Constantinople") under the terms of the Treaty of Sèvres. The international recognition of the GNAT and the [Government of Ankara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_the_Grand_National_Assembly "Government of the Grand National Assembly") was achieved through the signing of the [Treaty of Lausanne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne "Treaty of Lausanne") on 24 July 1923. The Grand National Assembly of Turkey promulgated the Republic on 29 October 1923.
12. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-35 "Jump up")** [Ottoman Turkish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language "Ottoman Turkish language"): دولت علیهٔ عثمانیه, [romanized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanization_of_Ottoman_Turkish "Romanization of Ottoman Turkish"): *Devlet-i ʿAlīye-i ʿOsmānīye*, [lit.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literal_translation "Literal translation") 'Sublime Ottoman State'; [Turkish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish_language "Turkish language"): *Osmanlı İmparatorluğu* or *Osmanlı Devleti*; [French](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_language "French language"): *Empire ottoman*<sup id="cite_ref-Strauss-2010_23-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Strauss-2010-23"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
13. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-38 "Jump up")** The [Ottoman dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty "Ottoman dynasty") also held the title "[caliph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_caliphs#Ottoman_Caliphate_\(1517%E2%80%931924\) "List of caliphs")" from the Ottoman victory over the [Mamluk Sultanate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate "Mamluk Sultanate") in [Ridaniya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Ridaniya "Battle of Ridaniya") (1517) to the [abolition of the Caliphate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abolition_of_the_Caliphate "Abolition of the Caliphate") (1924) by the Grand National Assembly of Turkey.
14. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-67 "Jump up")** A lock-hold on trade between western Europe and Asia is often cited as a primary motivation for [Isabella I of Castile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile "Isabella I of Castile") to fund [Christopher Columbus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Columbus "Christopher Columbus")'s westward journey to find a sailing route to Asia and, more generally, for European seafaring nations to explore alternative trade routes (e.g., K.D. Madan, *Life and travels of Vasco Da Gama* (1998), 9; I. Stavans, *Imagining Columbus: the literary voyage* (2001), 5; W.B. Wheeler and S. Becker, *Discovering the American Past. A Look at the Evidence: to 1877* (2006), 105). This traditional viewpoint has been attacked as unfounded in an influential article by A.H. Lybyer ("The Ottoman Turks and the Routes of Oriental Trade", *English Historical Review*, 120 (1915), 577–588), who sees the rise of Ottoman power and the beginnings of Portuguese and Spanish explorations as unrelated events. His view has not been universally accepted (cf. K.M. Setton, *The Papacy and the Levant (1204–1571), Vol. 2: The Fifteenth Century (Memoirs of the American Philosophical Society, Vol. 127)* (1978), 335).
15. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-181 "Jump up")** Though the revolt was officially initiated on the 10 June, bin Ali's sons ['Ali](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ali_of_Hejaz "Ali of Hejaz") and [Faisal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faisal_I_of_Iraq "Faisal I of Iraq") had already initiated operations at Medina starting on 5 June.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-180"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>166<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

As the Ottoman Turks learned Persian, the language and the culture it carried seeped not only into their court and imperial institutions but also into their vernacular language and culture. The appropriation of Persian, both as a second language and as a language to be steeped together with Turkish, was encouraged notably by the sultans, the ruling class, and leading members of the mystical communities.

Persian historical literature was first patronized during the reign of Mehmed II and continued unabated until the end of the 16th century. `{{[cite book](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Template:Cite_book "Template:Cite book")}}`: ISBN / Date incompatibility ([help](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#invalid_isbn_date "Help:CS1 errors"))

17. ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-Strauss-2010_23-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-Strauss-2010_23-1) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-Strauss-2010_23-2) Strauss, Johann (2010). ([info page on book](http://menadoc.bibliothek.uni-halle.de/urn/urn:nbn:de:gbv:3:5-91645) [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20190920231333/http://menadoc.bibliothek.uni-halle.de/urn/urn:nbn:de:gbv:3:5-91645) 20 September 2019 at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine") at [Martin Luther University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Luther_University "Martin Luther University")) // CITED: p. 26 (PDF p. 28): "French had become a sort of semi-official language in the Ottoman Empire in the wake of the *Tanzimat* reforms.\[...\] It is true that French was not an ethnic language of the Ottoman Empire. 18. ^ [Jump up to: <sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-Lambton-1995_25-0) [<sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-Lambton-1995_25-1) [<sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup>](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-Lambton-1995_25-2) [Lambton, Ann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ann_Lambton "Ann Lambton"); [Lewis, Bernard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernard_Lewis "Bernard Lewis") (1995). [*The Cambridge History of Islam: The Indian sub-continent, South-East Asia, Africa and the Muslim west*](https://books.google.com/books?id=4AuJvd2Tyt8C). Vol. 2. Cambridge University Press. p. 320. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-521-22310-2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-22310-2 "Special:BookSources/978-0-521-22310-2"). [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20230114151657/https://books.google.com/books?id=4AuJvd2Tyt8C) from the original on 14 January 2023. Retrieved 25 July 2015.
19. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-28 "Jump up")** Pamuk, Şevket (2000). [*A Monetary History of the Ottoman Empire*](https://books.google.com/books?id=Htk3Wn789EQC&pg=PA30). Cambridge University Press. pp. 30–31\. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\) "ISBN (identifier)") [0-521-44197-8](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-44197-8 "Special:BookSources/0-521-44197-8"). [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20230114151034/https://books.google.com/books?id=Htk3Wn789EQC&pg=PA30) from the original on 14 January 2023. Retrieved 21 October 2022. The Ottomans began to strike coins in the name of [Orhan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orhan "Orhan") Bey in 1326. These earliest coins carried inscriptions such as "the great Sultan, Orhan son of Osman" \[...\] Ottoman historiography has adopted 1299 as the date for the foundation of the state. 1299 might represent the date at which the Ottomans finally obtained their independence from the [Seljuk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seljuk_dynasty "Seljuk dynasty") sultan at [Konya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konya "Konya"). Probably, they were forced at the same time, or very soon thereafter, to accept the overlordship of the [Ilkhanids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ilkhanids "Ilkhanids") \[...\] Numismatic evidence thus suggest that independence did not really occur until 1326.

29. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-42 "Jump up")**
	- [Howard 2016](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFHoward2016), p. 45
	- [Somel 2010](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFSomel2010), p. xcvii
30. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-43 "Jump up")**
	- [Kaser 2011](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFKaser2011), p. 336: "The emerging Christian nation states justified the prosecution of their Muslims by arguing that they were their former "suppressors". The historical balance: between about 1820 and 1920, millions of Muslim casualties and refugees back to the remaining Ottoman Empire had to be registered; estimations speak about 5 million casualties and the same number of displaced persons"
	- [Fábos 2005](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFF%C3%A1bos2005), p. 437: "Muslims had been the majority in Anatolia, the Crimea, the Balkans, and the Caucasus and a plurality in southern Russia and sections of Romania. Most of these lands were within or contiguous with the Ottoman Empire. By 1923, 'only Anatolia, eastern Thrace, and a section of the southeastern Caucasus remained to the Muslim land ... Millions of Muslims, most of them Turks, had died; millions more had fled to what is today Turkey. Between 1821 and 1922, more than five million Muslims were driven from their lands. Five and one-half million Muslims died, some of them killed in wars, others perishing as refugees from starvation and disease' (McCarthy 1995, 1). Since people in the Ottoman Empire were classified by religion, Turks, Albanians, Bosnians, and all other Muslim groups were recognized—and recognized themselves—simply as Muslims. Hence, their persecution and forced migration is of central importance to an analysis of 'Muslim migration.'"
	- Schayegh, Cyrus (2024). "A Late/Post-Imperial Region of Difference: The Ottoman Empire and its Successor Polities in Southeastern Europe, Turkey, and the Arab East, c. 1850s–1940s". *Journal of World History*. **35** (4): 579–622\. [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_\(identifier\) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1353/jwh.2024.a943172](https://doi.org/10.1353%2Fjwh.2024.a943172). Between 1821 and the 1919–1922 Turko-Greek War, about five and a half million Muslims died of religious-ethnic war-related causes, including disease and hunger during forced migration, in southeastern Europe and the Crimea and Caucasus.
	- [Findley 2008](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFFindley2008), pp. 16, 35
	- [Kirişci 2008](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFKiri%C5%9Fci2008), pp. 175–177
	- [Karpat 2001](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFKarpat2001), p. 343: "The main migrations started from Crimea in 1856 and were followed by those from the Caucasus and the Balkans in 1862 to 1878 and 1912 to 1916. These have continued to our day. The quantitative indicators cited in various sources show that during this period a total of about 7 million migrants from Crimea, the Caucasus, the Balkans, and the Mediterranean islands settled in Anatolia. These immigrants were overwhelmingly Muslim, except for a number of Jews who left their homes in the Balkans and Russia in order to live in the Ottoman lands. By the end of the century the immigrants and their descendants constituted some 30 to 40 percent of the total population of Anatolia, and in some western areas their percentage was even higher." ... "The immigrants called themselves Muslims rather than Turks, although most of those from Bulgaria, Macedonia, and eastern Serbia descended from the Turkish Anatolian stock who settled in the Balkans in the fifteenth and sixteenth centuries."
	- [Karpat 2004](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFKarpat2004), pp. 5–6: "Migration was a major force in the social and cultural reconstruction of the Ottoman state in the nineteenth century. While some seven to nine million, mostly Muslim, refugees from lost territories in the Caucasus, Crimea, Balkans and Mediterranean islands migrated to Anatolia and Eastern Thrace, during the last quarter of the nineteenth and the early part of the twentieth centuries..."
	- [Pekesen 2012](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFPekesen2012): "The immigration had far-reaching social and political consequences for the Ottoman Empire and Turkey." ... "Between 1821 and 1922, some 5.3 million Muslims migrated to the Empire.50 It is estimated that in 1923, the year the republic of Turkey was founded, about 25 per cent of the population came from immigrant families.51"
	- [Biondich 2011](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFBiondich2011), p. 93: "The road from Berlin to Lausanne was littered with millions of casualties. In the period between 1878 and 1912, as many as two million Muslims emigrated voluntarily or involuntarily from the Balkans. When one adds those who were killed or expelled between 1912 and 1923, the number of Muslim casualties from the Balkan far exceeds three million. By 1923 fewer than one million remained in the Balkans"
	- [Armour 2012](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFArmour2012), p. 213: "To top it all, the Empire was host to a steady stream of Muslim refugees. Russia between 1854 and 1876 expelled 1.4 million Crimean Tartars, and in the mid-1860s another 600,000 Circassians from the Caucasus. Their arrival produced further economic dislocation and expense."
	- [Bosma, Lucassen & Oostindie 2012a](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#CITEREFBosmaLucassenOostindie2012a), p. 17: "In total, many millions of Turks (or, more precisely, Muslim immigrants, including some from the Caucasus) were involved in this 'repatriation' – sometimes more than once in a lifetime – the last stage of which may have been the immigration of seven hundred thousand Turks from Bulgaria between 1940 and 1990. Most of these immigrants settled in urban north-western Anatolia. Today between a third and a quarter of the Republic's population are descendants of these Muslim immigrants, known as Muhacir or Göçmen"

By the seventeenth century, literate circles in Istanbul would not call themselves Turks, and often, in phrases such as 'senseless Turks', used the word as a term of abuse.

The scholarly community specializing in Ottoman studies has of late virtually banned the use of "Turkey", "Turks", and "Turkish" from acceptable vocabulary, declaring "Ottoman" and its expanded use mandatory and permitting its "Turkish" rival only in linguistic and philological contexts.

Osman I, also called Osman Gazi, (born c. 1258—died 1324 or 1326), ruler of a Turkmen principality in northwestern Anatolia who is regarded as the founder of the Ottoman Turkish state.

Cambridge University Press. p. 9. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-1-107-41144-9](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-107-41144-9 "Special:BookSources/978-1-107-41144-9"). Ottomanist historians have produced several works in the last decades, revising the traditional understanding of this period from various angles, some of which were not even considered as topics of historical inquiry in the mid-twentieth century. Thanks to these works, the conventional narrative of Ottoman history – that in the late sixteenth century the Ottoman Empire entered a prolonged period of decline marked by steadily increasing military decay and institutional corruption – has been discarded.; Woodhead, Christine (2011). "Introduction". In Christine Woodhead (ed.). *The Ottoman World*. Routledge. p. 5. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-415-44492-7](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-415-44492-7 "Special:BookSources/978-0-415-44492-7"). Ottomanist historians have largely jettisoned the notion of a post-1600 'decline'

77. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-91 "Jump up")** Ágoston, Gábor (2009). "Introduction". In Ágoston, Gábor; Bruce Masters (eds.). *Encyclopedia of the Ottoman Empire*. p. xxxii.; Faroqhi, Suraiya (1994). "Crisis and Change, 1590–1699". In İnalcık, Halil; Donald Quataert (eds.). *An Economic and Social History of the Ottoman Empire, 1300–1914*. Vol. 2. Cambridge University Press. p. 553. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-521-57456-3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-57456-3 "Special:BookSources/978-0-521-57456-3"). In the past fifty years, scholars have frequently tended to view this decreasing participation of the sultan in political life as evidence for "Ottoman decadence", which supposedly began at some time during the second half of the sixteenth century. But recently, more note has been taken of the fact that the Ottoman Empire was still a formidable military and political power throughout the seventeenth century, and that noticeable though limited economic recovery followed the crisis of the years around 1600; after the crisis of the 1683–1699 war, there followed a longer and more decisive economic upswing. Major evidence of decline was not visible before the second half of the eighteenth century.

227. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-242 "Jump up")** Article 18 of the [Constitution of the Ottoman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Ottoman_Empire "Constitution of the Ottoman Empire") (1876)
228. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-243 "Jump up")** Davison, Roderic H. (31 December 1964). [*Reform in the Ottoman Empire, 1856–1876*](https://dx.doi.org/10.1515/9781400878765). [doi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doi_\(identifier\) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.1515/9781400878765](https://doi.org/10.1515%2F9781400878765). [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-1-4008-7876-5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4008-7876-5 "Special:BookSources/978-1-4008-7876-5"). [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20230114151544/https://www.degruyter.com/document/doi/10.1515/9781400878765/html) from the original on 14 January 2023. Retrieved 22 July 2021. There was the ruling Ottoman group, now largely concentrated in the bureaucracy centered on the Sublime Porte, and the mass of the people, mostly peasants. The efendi looked down on "the Turk," which was a term of opprobrium indicating boorishness, and preferred to think of himself as an Osmanli. His country was not Turkey, but the Ottoman State. His language was also "Ottoman"; though he might also call it "Turkish," in such a case he distinguished it from *kaba türkçe*, or coarse Turkish, the common speech. His writing included a minimum of Turkish words, except for particles and auxiliary verbs. `{{[cite book](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Template:Cite_book "Template:Cite book")}}`: ISBN / Date incompatibility ([help](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#invalid_isbn_date "Help:CS1 errors"))

[ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-8156-3342-6](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8156-3342-6 "Special:BookSources/978-0-8156-3342-6"). [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20230114151705/https://books.google.com/books?id=KhiQAwAAQBAJ) from the original on 14 January 2023. Retrieved 17 January 2016. In fact, one of the first Turkish works of fiction in Western-type novel form, Akabi Hikayesi (Akabi's Story), was written in Turkish by Vartan Pasha (born Osep/Hovsep Vartanian/Vartanyan, 1813–1879) and published in Armenian characters in 1851.
277. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-292 "Jump up")** Gürçaglar, Şehnaz; Paker, Saliha; Milton, John (2015). *Tradition, Tension, and Translation in Turkey*. John Benjamins Publishing Company. p. 5. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-90-272-6847-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-90-272-6847-1 "Special:BookSources/978-90-272-6847-1"). It is interesting that the first Ottoman novel in Turkish, Akabi Hikayesi (1851, Akabi's Story), was written and published in Armenian letters (for Armenian communities who read in Turkish) by Hovsep Vartanyan (1813–1879), known as Vartan Paşa, a leading Ottoman man of letters and journalist.

285. **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_ref-Bloom-2009_300-0 "Jump up")** M. Bloom, Jonathan; S. Blair, Sheila, eds. (2009). "Ottoman". *The Grove Encyclopedia of Islamic Art and Architecture*. Oxford University Press. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-19-530991-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-530991-1 "Special:BookSources/978-0-19-530991-1"). Throughout their history the Ottomans remained supporters of art and artists. Under their patronage a distinctive architectural style developed that combined the Islamic traditions of Anatolia, Iran and Syria with those of the Classical world and Byzantium. The result was a rationalist monumentality that favored spatial unity and architectonic expression.
